<td>2002</td>
<td>2002</td>
<td>2002</td>
<td>China, Indonesia
</td></tr></tbody></table><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>As it emerged from bankruptcy and company reorganization in 2010, GM structured its brand portfolio (<a href="/wiki/Brand_architecture" title="Brand architecture">brand architecture</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-merriamassociates.com_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merriamassociates.com-192">[191]</a></sup> Some nameplates like Pontiac, Saturn, Hummer, and service brands like Goodwrench were discontinued. Others, like <a href="/wiki/Saab_Automobile" title="Saab Automobile">Saab</a>, were sold.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup> The practice of putting the "GM Mark of Excellence" on every car, no matter what the brand, was discontinued in August 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> The company has moved from a corporate-endorsed hybrid brand architecture structure, where GM underpinned every brand to a multiple brand corporate invisible brand architecture structure.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> The company's familiar square blue "badge" has been removed from the Web site and advertising, in favor of a new, subtle all-text logo treatment on its U.S. site;<sup id="cite_ref-merriamassociates.com_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merriamassociates.com-192">[191]</a></sup> the Canadian site still retains the blue "badge".<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> In 2011, GM discontinued the Daewoo brand in South Korea and replaced it with the Chevrolet brand.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup>  GM holds about 96% of <a href="/wiki/GM_Korea" title="GM Korea">GM Korea</a> (2011–present) which mainly designs and produces Chevrolet and Holden branded vehicles. GM holds about 25% of
<a href="/wiki/GM_Uzbekistan" title="GM Uzbekistan">GM Uzbekistan</a> (2008–present) which produces Ravon, Chevrolet and Daewoo branded vehicles. 
</p><p>In February 2020, GM announced the retirement of the Holden brand due to poor sales. The brand will be completely phased out of both Australia and New Zealand, with cars ceasing to be sold from mid-2020, finally going defunct from 1 January 2021. It also announced that operations, such as servicing and extended warranties will be honoured for the next ten years. GM also announced that they would pull the Chevrolet brand from Thailand.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(11)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_conflicts">Labor conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Labor conflicts" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-11 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-11">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Flint_sit-down_strike">Flint sit-down strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Flint sit-down strike" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flint_sit-down_strike" title="Flint sit-down strike">Flint sit-down strike</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_sleeping.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_sleeping.jpg/220px-Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_sleeping.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="424"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 167px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_sleeping.jpg/220px-Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_sleeping.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="167" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_sleeping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young striker off sentry duty sleeping on the assembly line of auto seats</div></div></div>
<p>The 1936–1937 <a href="/wiki/Flint_sit-down_strike" title="Flint sit-down strike">Flint sit-down strike</a> against General Motors changed the <a href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" class="mw-redirect" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a> (UAW) from a collection of isolated <a href="/wiki/Local_union" title="Local union">locals</a> on the fringes of the industry into a major <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor union</a> and led to the unionization of the domestic <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> <a href="/wiki/Automobile_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile industry">automobile industry</a>.
</p><p>After the first convention of UAW in 1936, the union decided that it could not survive by piecemeal <a href="/wiki/Organizing_(management)" title="Organizing (management)">organizing</a> campaigns at smaller plants, as it had in the past, but that it could organize the automobile industry only by going after its biggest and most powerful employer, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors Corporation">General Motors Corporation</a>, focusing on GM's production complex in Flint, Michigan.
</p><p>Organizing in Flint was a difficult and dangerous plan. GM controlled city politics in Flint and kept a close eye on outsiders. According to <a href="/wiki/Wyndham_Mortimer" title="Wyndham Mortimer">Wyndham Mortimer</a>, the UAW officer put in charge of the organizing campaign in Flint, he received a death threat by an anonymous caller when he visited Flint in 1936. GM also maintained an extensive network of spies throughout its plants. This forced UAW members to keep the names of new members in secret and meeting workers at their homes.
</p><p>As the UAW studied its target, it discovered that GM had only two factories that produced the <a href="/wiki/Die_(manufacturing)" title="Die (manufacturing)">dies</a> from which car body components were stamped: one in Flint that produced the parts for <a href="/wiki/Buick" title="Buick">Buicks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pontiac" title="Pontiac">Pontiacs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile" title="Oldsmobile">Oldsmobiles</a>, and another in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland</a> that produced <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a> parts.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_National_Guard.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_National_Guard.jpg/220px-Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_National_Guard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="424"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 167px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_National_Guard.jpg/220px-Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_National_Guard.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="167" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flint_Sit-Down_Strike_National_Guard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/US_National_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="US National Guard">National Guardsmen</a> with machine guns overlooking Chevrolet factories number nine and number four</div></div></div>
<p>While the UAW called for a sit-down strike in Flint, the police, armed with guns and tear gas, attempted to enter the Fisher Body 2 plant on January 11, 1937. The strikers inside the plant pelted them with hinges, bottles, and bolts. At the time, <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Nance_Garner" title="John Nance Garner">John Nance Garner</a> supported federal intervention to break up the Flint Strike, but this idea was rejected by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>. The president urged GM to distinguish a union so the plants could re-open. The strike ended after 44 days.
</p><p>That development forced GM to bargain with the union. <a href="/wiki/John_L._Lewis" title="John L. Lewis">John L. Lewis</a>, President of the <a href="/wiki/United_Mine_Workers" title="United Mine Workers">United Mine Workers</a> and founder and leader of the Congress of Industrial Organizations, spoke for the UAW in those negotiations; UAW President <a href="/wiki/Homer_Martin_(labor_leader)" title="Homer Martin (labor leader)">Homer Martin</a> was sent on a speaking tour to keep him out of the way. GM's representatives refused to be in the same room as the UAW's, so Governor <a href="/wiki/Frank_Murphy" title="Frank Murphy">Frank Murphy</a> acted as a courier and <a href="/wiki/Mediation" title="Mediation">intermediary</a> between the two groups. Governor Murphy sent in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._National_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. National Guard">U.S. National Guard</a>, not to evict the strikers, but rather to protect them from the police and corporate <a href="/wiki/Strike-breaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Strike-breaker">strike-breakers</a>. The two parties finally reached agreement on February 11, 1937, on a one-page agreement that recognized the UAW as the exclusive bargaining representative for GM's employees who were members of the union for the next six months.<sup id="cite_ref-Bak_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bak-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Tool_and_die_strike_of_1939">Tool and die strike of 1939</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Tool and die strike of 1939" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tool_and_die_strike_of_1939" title="Tool and die strike of 1939">Tool and die strike of 1939</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tool_and_die_strike_of_1939" title="Tool and die strike of 1939">tool and die strike of 1939</a>, also known as the "strategy strike", was an ultimately successful attempt by the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="United Auto Workers Union">United Auto Workers Union</a> (UAW) to be recognized as the sole representative for General Motors workers. In addition to representation rights, the UAW, working jointly with the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Industrial_Organizations" title="Congress of Industrial Organizations">Congress of Industrial Organizations</a> (CIO), sought to resolve existing grievances of skilled workers.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="United_Auto_Workers_.28UAW.29_strike_of_1945-46"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Auto_Workers_(UAW)_strike_of_1945-46">United Auto Workers (UAW) strike of 1945-46</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: United Auto Workers (UAW) strike of 1945-46" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers_(UAW)_strike_of_1945-46" title="United Auto Workers (UAW) strike of 1945-46">United Auto Workers (UAW) strike of 1945-46</a></div>
<p>From November 21, 1945, until March 13, 1946 (113 days), <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Industrial_Organizations" title="Congress of Industrial Organizations">CIO</a>'s <a href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" class="mw-redirect" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a> (UAW), organized "320,000 hourly workers" to form a US-wide strike against the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors Corporation">General Motors Corporation</a>, workers used the tactic of the <a href="/wiki/Sit_down_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="Sit down strike">sit down strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnard_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnard-200">[199]</a></sup> It was "the longest strike against a major manufacturer" that the UAW had yet seen, and it was also "the longest national GM strike in its history."<sup id="cite_ref-Barnard_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnard-200">[199]</a></sup> As director of the UAW's General Motors Department (coordinator of union relations with GM),<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reuther" title="Walter Reuther">Walter Reuther</a> suggested to his colleagues the idea of striking the GM manufacturing plants with a 'one-at-a-time' strategy, which was "intended to maximize pressure on the target company."<sup id="cite_ref-Barnard_200-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnard-200">[199]</a></sup> Reuther also put forth the demands of the strikers: a 30 percent increase in wages and a hold on product prices. However, the strike ended to the dissatisfaction of Walter Reuther and the UAW, and the workers received only a 17.5-percent increase in wages.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2007_General_Motors_strike">2007 General Motors strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 2007 General Motors strike" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2007_General_Motors_strike" title="2007 General Motors strike">2007 General Motors strike</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2007_General_Motors_strike" title="2007 General Motors strike">2007 General Motors strike</a> was a strike from September 24 to 26, 2007, by the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers" title="United Auto Workers">United Auto Workers</a> against General Motors.
</p><p>On September 24, 2007, General Motors workers represented by the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers" title="United Auto Workers">United Auto Workers</a> union went on strike against the company.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup> The first US-wide strike against GM since 1970 was expected to idle 59 plants and facilities for an indefinite period of time. Talks broke down after more than 20 straight days of bargaining failed to produce a new contract. Major issues that proved to be stumbling blocks for an agreement included wages, benefits, job security and investments in US facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup></p><p>Within hours, the ripple effect was felt in Canada with closures of two car assembly plants in <a href="/wiki/Oshawa,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Oshawa, Ontario">Oshawa, Ontario</a>, and a transmission facility in <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor</a> on September 25. However, on September 26, a tentative agreement was reached, and the strike's end was announced by UAW officials in a news conference at 4 a.m.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> By the following day, all GM workers in both countries were back to work.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2019_General_Motors_strike">2019 General Motors strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 2019 General Motors strike" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2019_General_Motors_strike" title="2019 General Motors strike">2019 General Motors strike</a></div>
<p>On the morning of September 15, 2019, the United Auto Workers announced in a press conference that General Motors employees would begin striking at 11:59 PM after talks broke down to renew their contract, which expired earlier that day at 12:01 AM.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> This strike shut down operations in nine states, including 33 manufacturing plants and 22 parts distribution warehouses.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup>
After 40 days, the "longest strike by autoworkers in a decade" (and the longest against GM since 1970) came to an end on October 25, 2019 as United Auto Workers members voted to approve a new deal with GM, it cost GM more than $2bn and Union members have been getting by on $275 a week in strike pay.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(12)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Controversies" data-section="33" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-12 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-12">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Streetcar_conspiracy">Streetcar conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Streetcar conspiracy" data-section="34" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_streetcar_conspiracy" title="General Motors streetcar conspiracy">General Motors streetcar conspiracy</a></div>
<p>Between 1938 and 1950, General Motors (GM) monopolized the sale of buses and supplies to <a href="/wiki/National_City_Lines" title="National City Lines">National City Lines</a> (NCL) and its subsidiaries. It has been alleged that this was part of a deliberate plot to purchase and dismantle <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">streetcar</a> systems in many cities in the United States as an attempt to monopolize surface transportation.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ralph_Nader_and_the_Corvair">Ralph Nader and the Corvair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Ralph Nader and the Corvair" data-section="35" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1960-63_Corvair.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/1960-63_Corvair.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="90" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="90"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 120px;height: 90px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/1960-63_Corvair.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="120" data-height="90" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1960-63_Corvair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1961–63 Corvair swing-axle rear suspension</div></div></div>
<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Unsafe_at_Any_Speed" title="Unsafe at Any Speed">Unsafe at Any Speed</a></b></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a>, published in 1965, is a pioneering book accusing <a href="/wiki/Car_manufacturers" class="mw-redirect" title="Car manufacturers">car manufacturers</a> of being slow to introduce <a href="/wiki/Safety" title="Safety">safety</a> features, and reluctant to spend money on improving safety. The subject for which the book is probably most widely known, the rear-engined GM <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvair" title="Chevrolet Corvair">Chevrolet Corvair</a>, is covered in the first chapter. It relates to the first (1960–1964) models that had a <a href="/wiki/Swing_axle" title="Swing axle">swing-axle suspension</a> design which was prone to "tuck under" in certain circumstances. In substitution for the cost-cutting lack of a front stabilizer bar (anti-roll bar), Corvairs required <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">tire</a> pressures that were outside of the tire manufacturer's recommended tolerances. The Corvair relied on an unusually high front to rear pressure differential (15 psi front, 26 psi rear, when cold; 18 psi and 30 psi hot), and if one inflated the tires equally, as was standard practice for all other cars at the time, the result was dangerous oversteer.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup></p><p>In early March 1966, several media outlets, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, alleged that GM had tried to discredit <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a>, hiring private detectives to tap his phones and investigate his past, and hiring prostitutes to trap him in compromising situations.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup> Nader sued the company for <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_privacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of privacy">invasion of privacy</a> and settled the case for $425,000. Nader's lawsuit against GM was ultimately decided by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Court_of_Appeals" title="New York Court of Appeals">New York Court of Appeals</a>, whose opinion in the case expanded <a href="/wiki/Tort_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Tort law">tort law</a> to cover "overzealous surveillance".<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> Nader used the proceeds from the lawsuit to start the pro-consumer Center for Study of Responsive Law.
</p><p>A 1972 safety commission report conducted by <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University" title="Texas A&amp;M University">Texas A&amp;M University</a> concluded that the 1960–1963 Corvair possessed no greater potential for loss of control than its contemporary competitors in extreme situations.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Braithwaite_1983._p._30_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Braithwaite_1983._p._30-212">[211]</a></sup> The U.S. Department of Transportation (DOT) issued a press release in 1972 describing the findings of <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">NHTSA</a> testing from the previous year. NHTSA had conducted a series of comparative tests in 1971 studying the handling of the 1963 Corvair and four contemporary cars—a <a href="/wiki/Ford_Falcon_(North_America)" title="Ford Falcon (North America)">Ford Falcon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant" title="Plymouth Valiant">Plymouth Valiant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Beetle" title="Volkswagen Beetle">Volkswagen Beetle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renault_Dauphine" title="Renault Dauphine">Renault Dauphine</a>—along with a second-generation Corvair (with its completely redesigned, independent rear suspension). The 143-page report reviewed NHTSA's extreme-condition handling tests, national crash-involvement data for the cars in the test as well as General Motors' internal documentation regarding the Corvair's handling.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> NHTSA went on to contract an independent advisory panel of engineers to review the tests. This review panel concluded that "the 1960–63 Corvair compares favorably with contemporary vehicles used in the tests [...] the handling and stability performance of the 1960–63 Corvair does not result in an abnormal potential for loss of control or rollover, and it is at least as good as the performance of some contemporary vehicles both foreign and domestic."
</p><p>Former GM executive <a href="/wiki/John_DeLorean" title="John DeLorean">John DeLorean</a> asserted in his book <i>On a Clear Day You Can See General Motors</i> that Nader's criticisms were valid.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup></p><p>Journalist <a href="/wiki/David_E._Davis" title="David E. Davis">David E. Davis</a>, in a 2009 article in <i><a href="/wiki/Automobile_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile Magazine">Automobile Magazine</a></i>, noted that despite Nader's claim that swing-axle rear suspension were dangerous, <a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> all used similar swing-axle concepts during that era.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ignition_switch_recall">Ignition switch recall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Ignition switch recall" data-section="36" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_ignition_switch_recalls" title="General Motors ignition switch recalls">General Motors ignition switch recalls</a></div>
<p>In May 2014 the <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">NHTSA</a> fined the company $35 million for failing to recall cars with faulty ignition switches for a decade, despite knowing there was a problem with the switches. General Motors paid compensation for 124 deaths linked to the faulty switches.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup> The $35 million fine was the maximum the regulator could impose.<sup id="cite_ref-GMfined_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMfined-217">[216]</a></sup> General Motors has announced that they are also facing 79 customer lawsuits asking for as much as $10 billion for economic losses attributed to the recall.<sup id="cite_ref-GMlawsuits_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMlawsuits-218">[217]</a></sup> As well as the Cobalts, the switches of concern had been installed in many other cars, such as the Pontiac G5, the Saturn Ion, the Chevrolet HHR, the Saturn Sky, and Pontiac Solstice. Eventually the recall involved about 2.6 million GM cars worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="US_tariffs_on_non-US_built_vehicles">US tariffs on non-US built vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: US tariffs on non-US built vehicles" data-section="37" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Since January 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Donald_Trump" title="Presidency of Donald Trump">Trump Administration</a> had accused car manufacturers including General Motors of selling vehicles in the US market that were being imported/exported from other countries such as Canada, China, European Union, Japan, Mexico, South Korea, and Turkey. Due to this, President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> began imposing <a href="/wiki/Trump_tariffs" title="Trump tariffs">tariffs</a> on certain foreign-built vehicles (including the <a href="/wiki/Buick_Envision" title="Buick Envision">Buick Envision</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chevy_Blazer" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevy Blazer">Chevy Blazer</a>), which has negatively affected General Motors. Reasons for these tariffs were due to Trump's America First Policy and human right violations in some of those countries.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup></p>
<dl><dt>Chevy Blazer</dt></dl><p>The decision to build the Blazer in Mexico was criticized by the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers" title="United Auto Workers">United Auto Workers</a>, who wanted the vehicle built in the United States and is disappointed by GM's reason behind the announcement to begin production outside the country, citing GM's decision to cut back on its second shift workforce (and announced shutdown) at the <a href="/wiki/Lordstown_Assembly" title="Lordstown Assembly">Lordstown Assembly</a> and moving production to Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup> On January 10, 2019, the UAW announced that it has ordered its members to boycott the Blazer. Terry Dittes, Director of the UAW's GM Department, said the automaker has doubled down on cheaper labor in Mexico and continues to use temporary workers at U.S. plants, which robs laid-off UAW members of jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup></p><p>While GM had already decided on Ramos Arizpe prior to the development of the Blazer, the union, as well as the automotive industry in general, is concerned that the possibility of President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>’s statement about raising tariffs on vehicles built outside the United States and threats to force automakers to bring jobs back to the country (based on his <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> message made on June 22, 2018) could make things complicated, but GM has dismissed those theories and is confident about the Blazer’s prospects.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup></p><p>The move also gave concerns for lawmakers from both political parties. U.S. Congresswoman <a href="/wiki/Debbie_Dingell" title="Debbie Dingell">Debbie Dingell</a> (D-Michigan), herself a former executive at GM, said her former employer's decision to idle U.S. plants in 2019, on top of the decision to produce the Blazer in Mexico, reinforces a belief that the just-agreed <a href="/wiki/United_States%E2%80%93Mexico%E2%80%93Canada_Agreement" title="United States–Mexico–Canada Agreement">USMCA deal made by President Trump</a> doesn't correct the soon-to-be-replaced <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">NAFTA</a>'s problems: "Before I approve any trade agreement I am going to make sure we're writing trade agreements that level the playing field for the working men and women of this country, and that we're keeping jobs here, not making it easier to keep shipping jobs overseas... This GM announcement gives me greater concern, not less."<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup></p><p>On March 26, 2019, a Blazer and a <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado" title="Chevrolet Silverado">Chevrolet Silverado</a> were placed atop the Chevrolet Fountain at Detroit's <a href="/wiki/Comerica_Park" title="Comerica Park">Comerica Park</a>, as the official vehicles for the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a>' 2019 MLB baseball season. The Blazer was replaced a few days later by a Traverse, after complaints from automotive workers and enthusiasts in the local media, pointing out that it was made in Mexico, and the park's proximity to GM's Detroit-Hamtramck assembly, which is scheduled to go idle in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup></p>
<dl><dt>Buick Envision</dt></dl><p>The announcement about selling the Chinese-built vehicle to American consumers has drawn a backlash from the United Auto Workers, who had just finalized a contract agreement with GM and had wanted the automaker to build the Envision stateside: “Today’s announcement by General Motors that they are importing the Envision from China is a slap in the face to U.S. taxpayers and the men and women who worked so hard to save GM during its darkest time,” UAW President Dennis Williams and Vice President Cindy Estrada, who head the GM department for the union, said in a joint statement on December 4, 2015. On the other hand, Buick spokesman Stuart Fowle defended the decision to keep production in China and import it rather than having it built in the United States because it made sense from a business point of view: “We have production already going (in China) and adding a second manufacturing facility for the volumes that we would expect in the U.S. just wasn’t feasible,” he said. “We wanted to take advantage of the manufacturing already in place. We’re very confident in the vehicle.”<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup></p><p>On August 6, 2018, General Motors announced that it might withdraw the Envision from the United States market, should a request to seek a waiver to continue to import the vehicle is denied. GM is seeking the waiver amid a trade war between the United States and China over tariffs that could hurt the sales of the Envision. The automaker sees the potential exemption as “the only way” to continue offering the vehicle to U.S. consumers, according to GM President <a href="/wiki/Dan_Ammann" title="Dan Ammann">Dan Ammann</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[227]</a></sup> The exemption was denied on May 29, 2019, but GM has opted to pay the tariffs without raising the price on the vehicle for the time being. Buick is confident that ongoing talks between China and the United States could spare the Envision's future in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 10,</span> 2017</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=HybridCars.com&amp;rft.atitle=Nissan%E2%80%99s+Quarter-Millionth+Leaf+Means+It%E2%80%99s+The+Best-Selling+Plug-in+Car+In+History&amp;rft.date=2017-01-09&amp;rft.aulast=Cobb&amp;rft.aufirst=Jeff&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.hybridcars.com%2Fnissans-quarter-millionth-leaf-means-its-the-best-selling-plug-in-car-in-history%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGeneral+Motors" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"> <i>As of December 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the Nissan Leaf is the world's best-selling plug-in car in history with more than 250,000 units delivered, followed by the Tesla Model S with over 158,000 sales, the Volt/Ampera family of vehicles with 134,500 vehicles sold, and the Mitsubishi Outlander PHEV with about 116,500 units sold through November 2016. These are the only plug-in electric cars so far with over 100,000 global sales.</i></span>
